power.[[357]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-357)[[358]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-358)

### China

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Authoritarian_socialism&action=edit&section=21 "Edit section: China")]

[![Image 15](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Mao_Tse_Tung.jpg/250px-Mao_Tse_Tung.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Mao_Tse_Tung.jpg)

Mao Zedong

Following the fall of the elite, [land-owning class](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Landlord "Landlord") of the early 20th century, China began its [Communist Revolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chinese_Communist_Revolution "Chinese Communist Revolution") through the countryside. As relationships between agrarian masses and state-controlled programs splintered, the [Chinese Communist Party](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chinese_Communist_Party "Chinese Communist Party") led by Mao Zedong began seizing power.[[359]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Meeze-359) In his 1949 essay _On People's Democratic Dictatorship_, Mao committed himself and the Chinese state to the creation of a strong state power with increased economic control.[[359]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Meeze-359) He stressed the importance of an authoritarian state, where political order and unity could be established and maintained. Mao committed himself to unification in the vein of complete system overthrow.[[359]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Meeze-359) As [party chairman](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chairman_of_the_Chinese_Communist_Party "Chairman of the Chinese Communist Party"), Mao allowed himself complete control over the structure and execution of the party with his own [cult of personality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cult_of_personality "Cult of personality"), an almost mythical position as a guardian of wisdom and charisma.[[359]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Meeze-359)

With such power, Mao was able to influence popular opinions, allowing his agenda support without going through state-controlled measures. During the Great Leap Forward, an initiative to develop China from an agrarian sector a major industrial powerhouse, Mao relied greatly on his prestige to influence the people.[[359]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Meeze-359) However, the Great Leap Forward proved a failure as widespread crop and irrigation failures led to the 1959–1961 [Great Chinese Famine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_Chinese_Famine "Great Chinese Famine"). There was no suggested end to the revolution—it was meant to be a continuing process of empowerment of the [peasant class](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peasant_class "Peasant class").[[360]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Fetzer-360) With the aggressive failure of his [Cultural Revolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cultural_Revolution "Cultural Revolution"), Chinese support for the party and for Mao waned. Continuing struggles after his death would undermine his socialist system, allowing a more democratic yet still one-party ruled system to continue into today. As there is little agreement over his legacy both in China and abroad, Mao is a controversial figure who has been regarded as one of the most important and influential individuals in modern world history.[[361]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-361)[[362]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-362)

Supporters credit Mao with driving imperialism out of China,[[363]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-363)[[364]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-364) modernizing the nation and building it into a [world power](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_power "World power"), promoting the status of women and improving education and health care as well as increasing life expectancy as China's population grew from around 550 million to over 900 million under his leadership,[[365]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO'Brien2002254-365)[[366]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey2010327-366) among other achievements.[[367]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGao2008-367)[[368]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGaltungStenslie2014-368) Conversely, his regime has been called [autocratic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Autocratic "Autocratic") and totalitarian and condemned for bringing about mass repression and destroying religious and cultural artifacts and sites. It was additionally responsible for vast numbers of deaths, with estimates ranging from 30 to 70 million victims through starvation, prison labour and mass executions.[[369]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShort2001-369)[[370]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFenby2008351-370) While some critics argue that Mao was dismissive of the suffering and death caused by his policies, or that he was well aware that his policies would be responsible for the deaths of millions,[[371]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-371) others have disputed this.[[369]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShort2001-369)[[372]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuncan2009-372)[[373]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-373)

Praised as a political intellect, theorist, military strategist, poet and visionary,[[369]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShort2001-369) Mao has been variously described as a "great historical criminal", "both monster and a genius", who was also "a great force for good",[[209]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Biography-209) a "great leader in history" and a "great criminal"[[372]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuncan2009-372) as well as "one of the great tyrants of the twentieth century", comparable to [Adolf Hitler](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Adolf_Hitler "Adolf Hitler") and Joseph Stalin,[[374]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-FOOTNOTELynch2004230-374)[[375]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-375) with a death toll surpassing both.[[369]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShort2001-369)[[370]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFenby2008351-370) However, others reject those comparisons, arguing that whereas the deaths caused by Nazi Germany and the Soviet Union were largely systematic and deliberate, the overwhelming majority of the deaths under Mao were unintended consequences of famine, noting that the [landlord class](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mass_killings_of_landlords_under_Mao_Zedong "Mass killings of landlords under Mao Zedong") was not exterminated as a people due to his belief in redemption through thought reform. Mao has been compared to 19th-century Chinese reformers who challenged China's traditional beliefs in the era of China's clashes with [Western colonial powers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Western_colonialism "Western colonialism")[[369]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShort2001-369) as well as to [United States President](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_President "United States President")[Andrew Jackson](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Andrew_Jackson "Andrew Jackson").[[376]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-376)

Similarly, Maoist economics policies are controversial. Supporters argue that life expectancy greatly improved under Mao and that such policies rapidly industrialized China and laid the groundwork for the country's later rise to become an economic superpower.[[208]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-O'Brien,_Gao,_Ebrey,_Galtung-208) Critics argue that policies such as the Great Leap Forward and the Cultural Revolution were impediments to industrialization and modernization that delayed economic development and claim that [China's economy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Economy_of_China "Economy of China") underwent its rapid growth only after Maoist policies had been widely abandoned. All in all, both supporters and critics alike generally agree that the human cost has been staggering.[[209]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Biography-209)

#### Maoism

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Authoritarian_socialism&action=edit&section=22 "Edit section: Maoism")]

Maoism is an adapted Sino-centric version of [Marxism–Leninism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marxism%E2%80%93Leninism "Marxism–Leninism").[[377]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Esh-377) While believing in [democratic centralism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Democratic_centralism "Democratic centralism"), where party decisions are brought about by scrutiny and debate and then are binding upon all members of the party once implemented, Mao did not accept dissenters to the party's decisions.[[360]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Fetzer-360) Through the Cultural Revolution and the [Campaign to Suppress Counterrevolutionaries](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Campaign_to_Suppress_Counterrevolutionaries "Campaign to Suppress Counterrevolutionaries"), Mao attempted to purge any subversive idea—especially capitalist or Western threat—with heavy force, justifying his actions as the necessary way for the central authority to keep power.[[360]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Fetzer-360)

At the same time, Mao emphasized the importance of cultural heritage and individual choice as a way of creating this national unity. He described his ideal system as "a political situation in which there is both centralism and democracy, both discipline and freedom, both unity of purpose and personal ease of mind and liveliness to facilitate the socialist revolution".[[360]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Fetzer-360) While the system advocates contradiction, Mao believed the state above all could provide the masses with the tools for their own expression, but his own brand of self-expression was wholly manufactured, built largely on replacing traditional practices and artifacts of [Chinese culture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chinese_culture "Chinese culture") with his own. Through this, transformation of the people towards an internal party collectiveness was possible.[[360]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Fetzer-360)

Notably, Mao's authoritarianism was rooted in a collective bottom-up style of empowerment. In his system, the proletariat and peasantry were responsible for rising up against the bureaucracy and capital of the state.[[377]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Esh-377) Joining the peasant class with the bourgeoisie of the countryside (the land-holding, local farmers), the group was able to stifle the claims to power by the wealthier, urban landowners through the banner of [communism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Communism "Communism"). Only when this collection of [peasants](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peasant "Peasant") and [petty bourgeoisie](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Petty_bourgeoisie "Petty bourgeoisie") existed could Mao grow his own, custom bureaucracy.[[377]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Esh-377) Once this unity was established, Mao argued that the people were the ones who could control the state, but his government's intense control over the citizenry emphasizes the contradiction in his theory—a contradiction, he maintained, was a necessary reality of their specialized system.[[377]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Esh-377)

#### Post-Maoism

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Authoritarian_socialism&action=edit&section=23 "Edit section: Post-Maoism")]

Following the [Chinese economic reforms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chinese_economic_reforms "Chinese economic reforms") in the 1980s by [Deng Xiaoping](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deng_Xiaoping "Deng Xiaoping"), most then current and former authoritarian socialist regimes have followed the [Chinese model](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chinese_model "Chinese model") while only leaders such as [Kim Jong-il](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kim_Jong-il "Kim Jong-il") and [Mobutu Sese Seko](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mobutu_Sese_Seko "Mobutu Sese Seko") maintained their orthodox views. Countries such as Vietnam ([socialist-oriented market economy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Socialist-oriented_market_economy "Socialist-oriented market economy")) and more recently Cuba have followed the Chinese [socialist market economy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Socialist_market_economy "Socialist market economy"). With the [Great Recession](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_Recession "Great Recession"), the [Washington Consensus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Washington_Consensus "Washington Consensus") has been losing favour to the Beijing Consensus. According to [Joshua Kurlantzick](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Joshua_Kurlantzick "Joshua Kurlantzick"), the Chinese model "offer a viable alternative to the leading democracies. In many ways, their systems pose the most serious challenge to democratic capitalism since the rise of communism and fascism in the 1920s and early 1930s".[[254]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Kurlantzick_2013-254)

While arguing that "the 'China model' has become shorthand for [economic liberalization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Economic_liberalization "Economic liberalization") without political liberalization", Kurlantzick cautiones that "China's model of development is actually more complex. It builds on earlier, state-centered Asian models of development such as in South Korea and Taiwan, while taking uniquely Chinese steps designed to ensure that the Communist Party remains central to economic and political policy-making". Kurlantzick argues that "the Beijing government maintains a high degree of control over the economy, but it is hardly returning to socialism". China developed "a hybrid form of capitalism in which it has opened its economy to some extent, but it also ensures the government controls strategic industries, picks corporate winners, determines investments by state funds, and pushes the banking sector to support national champion firms". Although noting that "China privatized many state firms" in the 1980s and 1990s, he states that "the central government still controls roughly 120 companies. [...] Working through these networks, the Beijing leadership sets state priorities, gives signals to companies, and determines corporate agendas, but does so without the direct hand of the state appearing in public".[[254]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Kurlantzick_2013-254)

According to Kurlantzick, "government intervention in business is utilized, in a way not possible in a free-market democracy, to strengthen the power of the ruling regime and China's position internationally. [...] In short, the China model sees commerce as a means to promote national interests, and not just to empower (and potentially to make wealthy) individuals. And for over three decades, China's model of development has delivered staggering successes". Along with India, China is providing "virtually the only growth in the whole global economy" and in about thirty years the country has gone from a poor, mostly agrarian nation to the second-largest economy in the world.[[254]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Kurlantzick_2013-254)

### Arab world

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Authoritarian_socialism&action=edit&section=24 "Edit section: Arab world")]

See also: [Arab socialism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arab_socialism "Arab socialism")

Socialism was introduced into the Middle East in the form of [populist](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populist "Populist") policies designed to galvanize the working class into overthrowing [colonial powers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Colonial_powers "Colonial powers") and their domestic allies. These policies were held by authoritarian states interested in the rapid industrialisation and social equalisation of Arab nations and often were characterised by redistributive or protectionist economic policies, lower class mobilization, charismatic leaders and promises to improve national living standards.[[378]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-New_Authoritarianism-378) Those states were progressive in terms of the colonial development that had occurred thus far. They allowed important political and economic gains to be made by workers, encouraged land redistribution, unseated oligarchical political powers and implemented [import-substituting](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Import_substitution_industrialization "Import substitution industrialization") industrialisation development strategies.[[378]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-New_Authoritarianism-378)

With the collapse of the Eastern Bloc following the Revolutions of 1989 and the dissolution of the Soviet Union in December 1991 as well as the push for [democratization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Democratization "Democratization"), many Arab states have moved toward a model of fiscal discipline proposed by the [Washington Consensus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Washington_Consensus "Washington Consensus").[[378]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-New_Authoritarianism-378) Although authoritarian leaders of those states implemented democratic institutions during the 1980s and 1990s, their multi-party elections created an arena in which business elites could lobby for personal interests while largely silencing the lower class.[[378]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-New_Authoritarianism-378)[Economic liberalisation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Economic_liberalisation "Economic liberalisation") in these regions yielded economies that led to regimes built on the support of [rent-seeking](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rent-seeking "Rent-seeking") urban elites, with political opposition inviting the prospect of political marginalisation and even retaliation.[[378]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-New_Authoritarianism-378) Academics and political scientists have classified Ba'athist Syria as an Arab government that is based on the cold war era model of authoritarian socialism.[[a]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-387)

While some Trotskyists such as the [Committee for a Workers' International](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Committee_for_a_Workers%27_International "Committee for a Workers' International") have included Ba'athist Syria as an authoritarian socialist state when they have had a [nationalized](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nationalized "Nationalized") economy as [deformed workers' states](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deformed_workers%27_states "Deformed workers' states"),[[387]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-388)[[388]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-389) other socialists argued that the [neo-Ba'athists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neo-Ba%27athists "Neo-Ba'athists") promoted capitalists from within the party and outside their countries.[[389]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-390)

#### Resistance to democratisation

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Authoritarian_socialism&action=edit&section=25 "Edit section: Resistance to democratisation")]

[![Image 16](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/WOSM-WAGGGS-Arab_Region.svg/250px-WOSM-WAGGGS-Arab_Region.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:WOSM-WAGGGS-Arab_Region.svg)

[Arab world](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arab_world "Arab world")

A great deal of debate has been paid by the field of comparative politics to how the Arab region was able to avoid the [third wave of democratisation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Third_wave_of_democratisation "Third wave of democratisation"). A number of arguments have been offered by professionals in the field, ranging from a discussion of prerequisites for [democratisation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Democratisation "Democratisation") not supported by the [Arab culture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arab_culture "Arab culture") to a lack of democratic actors initiating the necessary democratic transition.

Marsha Pripstein Posusney argues that the "patriarchal and tribal mentality of the culture is an impediment to the development of pluralist values", rendering Arab citizens prone to accept patriarchal leaders and lacking the national unity that many argue is necessary for democratization to be successful.[[7]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Regimes_and_Resistance-7) Eva Bellin concedes that the prevalence of [Islam](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Islam "Islam") is a distinguishing factor of the region and therefore must contribute to the region's exceptionalism, "given Islam's presumed inhospitality to democracy".[[7]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Regimes_and_Resistance-7) Posusney argues that this "intrinsic incompatibility between democracy and Islam" remains unproven given that efforts to test this association quantitatively have failed to produce conclusive results.[[7]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Regimes_and_Resistance-7) Ethnic divisions in the area have also been cited as a factor as well as a weak civil society, a state-controlled economy, poverty, low literacy rates and inequality.[[7]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Regimes_and_Resistance-7)[[390]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Debating-391)

In his book _Debating Arab Authoritarianism: Dynamics and Durability in Nondemocratic Regimes_, Oliver Schlumberger has argued that there is in fact an international ambivalence toward authoritarianism in the Middle East given that stability is preferred over the uncertainty of democratisation due to the region's oil and gas supplies and the strategic importance of its geopolitical location.[[390]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Debating-391)

### Africa

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Authoritarian_socialism&action=edit&section=26 "Edit section: Africa")]

See also: [African socialism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/African_socialism "African socialism")

[![Image 17](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/L%C3%A9opold_Senghor%2C_Pic%2C_5.jpg/250px-L%C3%A9opold_Senghor%2C_Pic%2C_5.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:L%C3%A9opold_Senghor,_Pic,_5.jpg)

[Léopold Sédar Senghor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/L%C3%A9opold_S%C3%A9dar_Senghor "Léopold Sédar Senghor")

During the [1945 Pan-African Conference](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pan-African_Congress "Pan-African Congress"), calls for increased organization, development and self-determination in the poverty stricken African continent put the impetus on colonial powers to negotiate [national sovereignty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_sovereignty "National sovereignty").[[5]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Mushkat-5) While there were few Marxist movements into the continent, Soviet Union activity spurred anti-imperialist and globalization movements from African countries. The congress established [national liberation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/African_independence_movements "African independence movements") as the main topic of their sessions, emphasizing the elimination and exploitation by the imperialist powers over authentic national sovereignty. However, they did not establish clear social or political parameters for this new liberation.[[5]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Mushkat-5)

African leaders consistently viewed socialism as a direct rejection of the [colonial system](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Colonisation_of_Africa "Colonisation of Africa") and in turn dismissed the notion of creating independent capitalist systems throughout the continent. They attempted to infuse various forms of socialism—some Marxist–Leninist, others democratic—into tailored ideologies specific to each country.[[5]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Mushkat-5) Once these systems were in place, countries developed towards a "focal institutional" society. According to sociologist William Friedland, societies adopted a totalitarian vision of rule, allowing one-party systems and institutions to "penetrate every sphere of private or public activity".[[6]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Gregor-6)

#### Senegal

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Authoritarian_socialism&action=edit&section=27 "Edit section: Senegal")]

Senegalese President [Leopold Sedar Senghor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Leopold_Sedar_Senghor "Leopold Sedar Senghor") was among the first and most vocal African advocates for socialism. Before being elected president, Senghor served as one of nine African delegates to the 1945 French Constituent Assembly, negotiating for the transfer of self-governing and policy-making power through locally elected councils.[[391]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-France-392) The measure shortly failed, keeping autonomy from the colonies until the [independence movements](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/French_colonial_empire#Decolonization "French colonial empire") of the 1960s.

After [Senegalese independence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Senegal#Independence "History of Senegal") in 1960, Senghor's [Union Progresiste Senegalaise](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Socialist_Party_of_Senegal "Socialist Party of Senegal"), a derivative of the [French Socialist Party](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/French_Socialist_Party "French Socialist Party"), grew massive support throughout the continent.[[6]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Gregor-6) Much of his party's success hinged on his revisionist version of Marxism–Leninism, where he argued that "the major contradiction of Marxism is that it presents itself as a science, whereas, despite its denials, it is based on an ethic".[[6]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Gregor-6) By framing it as an ethic, Senghor was able to remove the strict determinism from the ideology, allowing it to be molded towards an Afro-centric model. His revision proved similar to that of Benito Mussolini as he called on a national movement from and for his one-party-ruled government, arguing: "In a word, we must awaken the National Consciousness. [...] But the government cannot and must not do it all. It must be helped by the party. [...] Our party must be the consciousness of the masses".[[6]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Gregor-6)

#### Ghana

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Authoritarian_socialism&action=edit&section=28 "Edit section: Ghana")]

In the same vein as Senghor, socialist leader [Kwame Nkrumah](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah "Kwame Nkrumah") sought to advance this one-party, nationalized form of socialist obedience. Nkrumah stressed the importance of government-owned property and resources. He maintained that "production for private profit deprives a large section of the people of the goods and services produced", advocating public ownership to fit the "people's needs".[[392]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Kwame-393) To accomplish this, Nkrumah emphasized the importance of discipline and obedience towards the single socialist party. He argued that if people submitted and accepted the singular party's program, political independence would be possible.[[392]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Kwame-393) By 1965, his one-party rule had produced an Assembly entirely made up of his own party members.[[393]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Bretton-394)

Nkrumah saw law as a malleable weapon of political power, not as a product of a complex system of political institutions.[[393]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Bretton-394) Ghanaian power structures were dominated and controlled by his hand, but elite landowners questioned the legitimacy of Nkrumah's power. Those elites were only afforded one choice, namely to align with their government if they wanted access to the state. Gradually, those who were not granted or did not desire entrance into the party created regions blocs.[[394]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Redl-395) The [Asante](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ashanti_people "Ashanti people") emerged as a regional force capable political sway. With the power to set the agenda, the authoritarian party often clashed with these emerging regional groups, ultimately undermining the one-party system.[[394]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Redl-395)

#### Tanzania

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Authoritarian_socialism&action=edit&section=29 "Edit section: Tanzania")]

[Julius Nyerere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Julius_Nyerere "Julius Nyerere") attempted to socialist reform for [Tanzania](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tanzania "Tanzania") following those in [Ghana](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ghana "Ghana") and [Senegal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Senegal "Senegal"). The tenets of his initiatives were to promote the Tanzanian economy; secure state control over development; create a sole political party called the [Tanganyika African National Union](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tanganyika_African_National_Union "Tanganyika African National Union") (TANU) which would be under his control; and share the benefits of all gathered income.[[395]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Pratt-396)

The system—called ujaama—became a tool for nationalization of the [Tanzanian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tanzanian "Tanzanian") people. In the system, all Tanzanains were encouraged to run for office, with no campaign funding allowed. Speeches in the election would not focus on the national issues, but rather on the quality of the individual, each of whom would be closely controlled by TANU.[[395]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Pratt-396) Structurally, the power was shared along regional boundaries, giving increased policy making power and resource allocation to these regions. Local institutions were downplayed, with leadership organizations often facing subversion from higher governmental structures.[[396]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-397)

The first wave of elections in the [Tanzanian general election](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1965_Tanzanian_general_election "1965 Tanzanian general election") produced a 100% voting rate for TANU officials.[[397]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-398)[[398]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-399)

### Latin America

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Authoritarian_socialism&action=edit&section=30 "Edit section: Latin America")]

See also: [Pink tide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pink_tide "Pink tide")

[Socialism of the 21st century](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Socialism_of_the_21st_century "Socialism of the 21st century") is an interpretation of socialist principles first advocated by German sociologist and political analyst [Heinz Dieterich](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heinz_Dieterich "Heinz Dieterich") and taken up by a number of [Latin American](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Latin_Americans "Latin Americans") leaders. Dieterich argued in 1996 that both [free-market](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Free-market "Free-market")[industrial](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Industrialisation "Industrialisation") capitalism and 20th-century authoritarian socialism have failed to solve urgent problems of [humanity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Humanitarianism "Humanitarianism") like poverty, [hunger](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hunger "Hunger"), [exploitation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Exploitation_of_labour "Exploitation of labour"), [economic oppression](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Economic_oppression "Economic oppression"), [sexism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexism "Sexism"), [racism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Racism "Racism"), the destruction of [natural resources](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Natural_resource "Natural resource") and the absence of a truly [participatory democracy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Participatory_democracy "Participatory democracy").[[399]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Dieterich-400) While having democratic socialist elements, it primarily resembles [Marxist revisionism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Revisionism_(Marxism) "Revisionism (Marxism)").[[400]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Burbach-401) Leaders who have advocated for this form of socialism include [Hugo Chávez](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hugo_Ch%C3%A1vez "Hugo Chávez") of [Venezuela](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Venezuela "Venezuela"), [Néstor Kirchner](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/N%C3%A9stor_Kirchner "Néstor Kirchner") of [Argentina](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Argentina "Argentina"), [Rafael Correa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rafael_Correa "Rafael Correa") of [Ecuador](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ecuador "Ecuador"), [Evo Morales](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evo_Morales "Evo Morales") of [Bolivia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bolivia "Bolivia") and [Luiz Inácio Lula da Silva](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Luiz_In%C3%A1cio_Lula_da_Silva "Luiz Inácio Lula da Silva") of [Brazil](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brazil "Brazil").[[401]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-PT-402) Because of the local unique historical conditions, it is often contrasted with previous applications of socialism in other countries, with a major difference being the effort towards a more decentralised and participatory planning process.[[400]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Burbach-401)

Critics claim that this form of socialism in Latin America acts as a façade for authoritarianism. The charisma of figures like Hugo Chávez and mottoes such as "Country, Socialism, or Death!" have drawn comparisons to the Latin American dictators and _[caudillos](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Caudillo "Caudillo")_ of the past.[[402]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-403) According to [Steven Levitsky](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Steven_Levitsky "Steven Levitsky"), only under "the dictatorships of the past [...] were presidents reelected for life", with Levitsky further stating that while Latin America experienced democracy, citizens opposed "indefinite reelection, because of the dictatorships of the past".[[8]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-CSMecuador-8) Levitsky then noted how in Ecuador, Nicaragua and Venezuela "reelection is associated with the same problems of 100 years ago".[[8]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-CSMecuador-8)

In 2014, _[The Washington Post](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The\_Washington\_Post "The Washington Post")_ also argued that "Bolivia's Evo Morales, David Ortega of Nicaragua and the late Venezuelan president Hugo Chávez [...] used the ballot box to weaken or eliminate term limits".[[403]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-404) The sustainability and stability of economic reforms associated with governments adhering to such socialism have also been questioned. Latin American countries have primarily financed their [social programs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bolivarian_missions "Bolivarian missions") with extractive exports like petroleum, natural gas and minerals, creating a dependency that some economists claim has caused inflation and slowed growth.[[404]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-405) While some critics say the crisis is caused by socialism or the country's socialist policies,[[405]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-406) its policies have been described as[_[by whom?](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual\_of\_Style/Words\_to\_watch#Unsupported\_attributions "Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch")_] populist[[406]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-407)[[407]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-408) or "hyper-populist"[[408]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-409) and the crisis has more to do with authoritarianism as well as anti-democratic governance, corruption and mismanagement of the economy.[[409]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-410)[[410]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-411)[[411]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-412)[[412]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-413) According to analysts and critics alike, the Bolivarian government has used those populist policies in order to maintain political power.[[413]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-414)[[414]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-415)[[415]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-416)

Although socialists have welcomed a socialism of the 21st century, they have been skeptical of Latin America's examples and criticized their authoritarian qualities and occasional cults of personality. While citing their progressive role, they argue that the appropriate label for these governments is _populism_ rather than _socialism_.[[416]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-417)[[417]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-418)[[418]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-419)[[419]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-420)[[420]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-421) Chávez and Maduro have been compared to Lenin and Stalin, respectively, including Chávez and Lenin's early deaths and the economic problems after their deaths. Maduro, who has joked about his similar appearance and [walrus moustache](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Walrus_moustache "Walrus moustache") with Stalin,[[421]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-422) argued he is not a new Stalin and claimed to be merely following Chávez.[[422]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-423) Nonetheless, Maduro has been variously described by newspapers such as the _[New Statesman](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New\_Statesman "New Statesman")_ and _[The Times](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The\_Times "The Times")_ as the "Stalin of the Caribbean" and the "tropical Stalin", respectively.[[423]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-424)[[424]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-425) According to _[The Daily Beast](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The\_Daily\_Beast "The Daily Beast")_, Maduro has embraced the "tropical Stalin" moniker.[[425]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-426) According to Joshua Kurlantzick, Latin American countries such as Nicaragua and Venezuela have been following the Chinese model and are described as authoritarian capitalist regimes.[[254]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Kurlantzick_2013-254)

#### Venezuela

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Authoritarian_socialism&action=edit&section=31 "Edit section: Venezuela")]

See also: [Crisis in Venezuela](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crisis_in_Venezuela "Crisis in Venezuela")

[![Image 18](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Hugo_Ch%C3%A1vez_salute.jpg/250px-Hugo_Ch%C3%A1vez_salute.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Hugo_Ch%C3%A1vez_salute.jpg)

[Hugo Chávez](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hugo_Ch%C3%A1vez "Hugo Chávez")

Venezuela under Chávez and his [Bolivarian Revolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bolivarian_Revolution "Bolivarian Revolution") moved toward authoritarian socialism.[[426]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Routledge-427)[[427]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Harrison-428) Chávez campaigned for a constituent assembly and to draft a new constitution, which was approved in 1999. The [1999 Venezuelan constitution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Constitution_of_Venezuela "Constitution of Venezuela") eliminated much of Venezuela's [checks and balances](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Separation_of_powers "Separation of powers"), Chávez's government controlled every branch of the Venezuelan government for over 15 years after it passed until the [2015 parliamentary election](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2015_Venezuelan_parliamentary_election "2015 Venezuelan parliamentary election").[[428]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-FH1999-429)[[429]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-430) The 1999 constitution also brought the military closer to political power, allowing military officers the right to vote, eliminating its apolitical nature, and transferring the function of military promotions of high officers to the president, which in the 1961 constitution was the responsibility of the [Senate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Senate_of_Venezuela "Senate of Venezuela").[[430]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-431) By January 2007, after being reelected in the [2006 presidential election](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2006_Venezuelan_presidential_election "2006 Venezuelan presidential election") and swearing in as president, Chávez began openly proclaiming the ideology of socialism of the 21st century.[[431]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-432) The Bolivarian government used "centralized decision-making and a top-down approach to policy formation, the erosion of vertical power-sharing and concentration of power in the presidency, the progressive deinstitutionalization at all levels, and an increasingly paternalist relationship between state and society" in order to hasten changes in Venezuela.[[432]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-433) In practice, Chávez's administration proposed and enacted [populist](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Economic_populism "Economic populism") economic policies.[[433]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-434)[[434]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-435)

Using record-high oil revenues of the 2000s, his government nationalized key industries, created [communal councils](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Venezuelan_communal_councils "Venezuelan communal councils") and implemented social programs known as the Bolivarian missions to expand access to food, housing, healthcare and education.[[435]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-436)[[436]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Devereux&Collitt-437)[[437]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-438)[[438]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-439)[[439]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-440)[[440]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-441)[[441]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-442)[[442]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-443)[[443]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-444) Venezuela received high oil profits in the mid-2000s,[[444]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Nagel-445) resulting in improvements in areas such as poverty, literacy, income equality and quality of life occurring primarily between 2003 and 2007.[[436]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Devereux&Collitt-437)[[444]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Nagel-445)[[445]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-446) However, those gains started to reverse after 2012 and it has been argued that government policies did not address [structural inequalities](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Structural_inequalities "Structural inequalities").[[446]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-447)

On 2 June 2010, Chávez declared an economic war due to [shortages in Venezuela](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shortages_in_Venezuela "Shortages in Venezuela"), beginning the [crisis in Bolivarian Venezuela](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crisis_in_Bolivarian_Venezuela "Crisis in Bolivarian Venezuela").[[447]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-448) By the end of Chávez's presidency in the early 2010s, economic actions performed by his government during the preceding decade such as [deficit spending](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deficit_spending "Deficit spending")[[448]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-449)[[449]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-450)[[450]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-451)[[451]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-452)[[452]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-453) and [price controls](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Price_control "Price control")[[453]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-454)[[454]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-ECONfood-455)[[455]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-456)[[456]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-457)[[457]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-458) proved to be unsustainable, with the [economy of Venezuela](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Economy_of_Venezuela "Economy of Venezuela") faltering while poverty,[[436]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Devereux&Collitt-437)[[444]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Nagel-445)[[458]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-459)[inflation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inflation "Inflation")[[454]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-ECONfood-455) and [shortages](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shortage "Shortage") increased. His use of [enabling acts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Enabling_act "Enabling act")[[459]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-460) and his government's use of [Bolivarian propaganda](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bolivarian_propaganda "Bolivarian propaganda") were also controversial.[[460]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-461)[[461]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-462)[[462]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-463)[[463]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-464) On the socialist development in Venezuela, Chávez argued with the second government plan (_[Plan de la Patria](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Plan\_de\_la\_Patria&action=edit&redlink=1 "Plan de la Patria (page does not exist)")[[es](https://es.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plan\_de\_la\_Patria "es:Plan de la Patria")]_) that "socialism has just begun to implant its internal dynamism among us" whilst acknowledging that "the socio-economic formation that still prevails in Venezuela is [capitalist and rentier](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rentier_capitalism "Rentier capitalism")".[[464]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-465)[_[non-primary source needed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:No\_original\_research#Primary,\_secondary\_and\_tertiary\_sources "Wikipedia:No original research")_] This same thesis is defended by Maduro.[[465]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-466)[_[non-primary source needed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:No\_original\_research#Primary,\_secondary\_and\_tertiary\_sources "Wikipedia:No original research")_]

[![Image 19](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Nicol%C3%A1s_Maduro%2C_president_of_Venezuela_%282016%29_cropped.jpg/250px-Nicol%C3%A1s_Maduro%2C_president_of_Venezuela_%282016%29_cropped.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Nicol%C3%A1s_Maduro,_president_of_Venezuela_(2016)_cropped.jpg)

[Nicolás Maduro](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nicol%C3%A1s_Maduro "Nicolás Maduro")

In 2015, _[The Economist](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The\_Economist "The Economist")_ argued that the Bolivarian Revolution in Venezuela—now under [Nicolás Maduro](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nicol%C3%A1s_Maduro "Nicolás Maduro") after [Chávez's death](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Death_of_Hugo_Ch%C3%A1vez "Death of Hugo Chávez") in 2013—was devolving from authoritarianism to dictatorship as opposition politicians were jailed for plotting to undermine the government, violence was widespread and independent media shut down.[[466]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-467)[Chávez](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Economic_policy_of_the_Hugo_Ch%C3%A1vez_administration "Economic policy of the Hugo Chávez administration") and [Maduro](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Economic_policy_of_the_Nicol%C3%A1s_Maduro_administration "Economic policy of the Nicolás Maduro administration") administrations' economic policies led to shortages, a high inflation rate and a dysfunctional economy.[[467]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-468) The government has attributed Venezuela's economic problems to the decline in oil prices, sanctions imposed by the United States and economic sabotage by the opposition.[[468]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-469) Western media coverage of Chávez and other Latin American leaders from the 21st-century socialist movement has been criticized as unfair by their supporters and left-leaning media critics.[[469]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-470)[[470]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-471)

Broadly, _[chavismo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chavismo "Chavismo")_ policies include nationalization, [social welfare](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Social_welfare "Social welfare") programs and [opposition to neoliberalism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Opposition_to_neoliberalism "Opposition to neoliberalism"), particularly the policies of the [International Monetary Fund](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Monetary_Fund "International Monetary Fund") and the [World Bank](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_Bank "World Bank"). According to Chávez, Venezuelan socialism accepts [private property](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Private_property "Private property"),[[471]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-472) but this socialism is a form of social democracy that seeks to promote [social property](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Social_property "Social property").[[472]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-473) In January 2007, Chávez proposed to build the communal state, whose main idea is to build self-government institutions like communal councils, communes and communal cities.[[473]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-474) While Chávez remained relatively popular throughout his time in office, Maduro suffered unpopularity with the [deterioration of the economy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2013%E2%80%93present_economic_crisis_in_Venezuela "2013–present economic crisis in Venezuela") during his tenure and there was a decline of self-identified _chavistas_.[[474]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-475)[[475]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-476)[[476]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-477)

Despite its socialist rhetoric, _chavismo_ has been frequently described as being [state capitalist](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/State_capitalist "State capitalist") by critics.[[477]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-478)[[478]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-479) Critics frequently point towards Venezuela's large private sector.[[479]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-480) In 2009, roughly 70% of Venezuela's [gross domestic product](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gross_domestic_product "Gross domestic product") was created by the private sector.[[480]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-481) According to Asa Cusack, an expert on Latin America and frequent contributor to [mainstream media](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mainstream_media "Mainstream media"), Venezuela's economy remained "market-based and private-sector dominated" throughout Chávez's time in office. Although "the social economy and the public sector were heavily promoted", for example through nationalization, "the private sector was expected to remain dominant, and it did. A centrally planned socialist economy like Cuba's was neither the aim nor the reality".[[481]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-482)_Chavismo_ has been widely discussed in the media.[[482]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-483)[[483]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-484)[[484]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-485)[[485]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-486)[[486]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-487)[[487]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-488)[[488]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-489)[[489]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-490)

According to Kirk A. Hawkins, scholars are generally divided into two camps, namely a liberal-democratic one that sees _chavismo_ as an instance of [democratic backsliding](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Democratic_backsliding "Democratic backsliding") and a [radical-democratic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radical-democratic "Radical-democratic") one that upholds _chavismo_ as the fulfillment of its aspirations for participatory democracy. Hawkins argues that the most important division between these two groups is neither methodological nor theoretical, but ideological. It is a division over basic normative views of democracy, i.e. [liberalism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Liberalism "Liberalism") versus [radicalism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Political_radicalism "Political radicalism"). Scholars in the first camp tended to adhere to a [classical liberal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Classical_liberal "Classical liberal") ideology that valued procedural democracy (competitive elections, [widespread participation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Voter_turnout "Voter turnout") defined primarily in terms of voting and [civil liberties](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Civil_liberties "Civil liberties")) as the political means best suited to achieving human welfare. They saw _chavismo_ in a mostly negative light as a case of democratic backsliding or even competitive authoritarianism or electoral authoritarian regime. On the other hand, scholars in the second camp generally adhered to a classical socialist ideology that mistrusted market institutions in either the state or the economy. Although accepting the importance of liberal-democratic institutions, they saw procedural democracy as insufficient to ensure political inclusion and emphasized participatory forms of democracy and collective worker ownership in the economy.[[490]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-491)

Criticism
---------

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Authoritarian_socialism&action=edit&section=32 "Edit section: Criticism")]

Main article: [Criticism of communist party rule](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Criticism_of_communist_party_rule "Criticism of communist party rule")

### Left-wing

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Authoritarian_socialism&action=edit&section=33 "Edit section: Left-wing")]

See also: [Anti-Leninism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anti-Leninism "Anti-Leninism") and [Anti-Stalinist left](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anti-Stalinist_left "Anti-Stalinist left")

Left-wing critics argue that it is a form of state capitalism that followed [anti-imperialism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anti-imperialism "Anti-imperialism"), [populism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism "Populism"), [nationalism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nationalism "Nationalism") and social democracy. Rather than representing a socialist planned economy, the Soviet model has been described in practice as either a form of state capitalism[[138]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Chomsky_1986-138)[[139]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-State_Capitalism_in_the_Soviet_Union,_2001-139)[[140]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWolff2015-140) or a [non-planned](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Administrative-command_system "Administrative-command system"), command economy.[[69]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-The_Soviet_Union_Has_an_Administered,_Not_a_Planned,_Economy,_1985-69)[[147]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Zimbalist_&_Sherman_1988-147)[[70]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Ellman_2007-70) The fidelity of those varied socialist revolutionaries, leaders and parties to the work of Karl Marx and that of other socialist thinkers is highly contested and has been rejected by many Marxists and other socialists alike.[[491]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-492) Some academics, scholars and socialists have criticized the linking of all leftist and socialist ideals to the excesses of authoritarian socialism.[[492]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-493)

#### Anarchism and Marxism

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Authoritarian_socialism&action=edit&section=34 "Edit section: Anarchism and Marxism")]

Many democratic and libertarian socialists, including [anarchists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anarchists "Anarchists"), [mutualists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mutualism_(economic_theory) "Mutualism (economic theory)") and [syndicalists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Syndicalists "Syndicalists"), deride it as state socialism for its support of a workers' state instead of abolishing the bourgeois state apparatus outright. They use the term in contrast with their own form of socialism which involves either [collective ownership](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Collective_ownership "Collective ownership") (in the form of [worker cooperatives](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Worker_cooperative "Worker cooperative")) or [common ownership](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Common_ownership "Common ownership") of the [means of production](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Means_of_production "Means of production") without [central planning](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Central_planning "Central planning") by the state. Those [libertarian socialists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libertarian_socialists "Libertarian socialists") believe there is no need for a state in a socialist system because there would be no class to suppress and no need for an institution based on coercion and regard the state being a remnant of capitalism.[[493]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-494)[[494]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-AFAQ_H-495)[[495]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-AFAQ_I-496) They hold that statism is antithetical to true socialism,[[25]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-McKay_2008-25) the goal of which is the eyes of libertarian socialists such as [William Morris](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/William_Morris "William Morris"), who wrote as follows in a _Commonweal_ article: "State Socialism? — I don't agree with it; in fact I think the two words contradict one another, and that it is the business of Socialism to destroy the State and put Free Society in its place".[[496]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-497)

Classical and orthodox Marxists also view the term as an oxymoron, arguing that while an association for managing production and economic affairs would exist in socialism, it would no longer be a state in the [Marxist definition](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marx%27s_theory_of_the_state "Marx's theory of the state") which is based on [domination by one class](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anti-statism#General_categories "Anti-statism"). Preceding the Bolshevik-led revolution in Russia, many socialist groups—including reformists, orthodox Marxist currents such as [council communism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Council_communism "Council communism") and the [Mensheviks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mensheviks "Mensheviks") as well as anarchists and other libertarian socialists—criticized the idea of using the state to conduct planning and nationalization of the means of production as a way to establish socialism.[[497]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-498) Lenin himself acknowledged his policies as state capitalism,[[258]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Lenin1-258)[[259]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Lenin2-259)[[260]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Lenin3-260)[[261]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Pena-261) defending them from left-wing criticism,[[498]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-499) but arguing that they were necessary for the future development of socialism and not socialist in themselves.[[499]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-500)[[500]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-501)

American Marxist [Raya Dunayevskaya](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Raya_Dunayevskaya "Raya Dunayevskaya") dismissed it as a type of state capitalism[[501]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-502)[[502]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-503)[[503]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-504) because state ownership of the means of production is a form of state capitalism;[[504]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-505) the [dictatorship of the proletariat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dictatorship_of_the_proletariat "Dictatorship of the proletariat") is a form of democracy and [single-party rule](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Single-party_rule "Single-party rule") is undemocratic;[[505]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-506) and Marxism–Leninism is neither [Marxism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marxism "Marxism") nor Leninism, but rather a composite ideology which socialist leaders such as Joseph Stalin used to expediently determine what is communism and what is not communism among the Eastern Bloc countries.[[506]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-507)

#### Left communism

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Authoritarian_socialism&action=edit&section=35 "Edit section: Left communism")]

Critical of the economy and government of socialist states, [left communists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Left_communism "Left communism") such as the Italian [Amadeo Bordiga](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amadeo_Bordiga "Amadeo Bordiga") argued that Marxism–Leninism was a form of political opportunism which preserved rather than destroyed capitalism because of the claim that the exchange of commodities would occur under [socialism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Socialist_mode_of_production "Socialist mode of production"); the use of [popular front](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Popular_front "Popular front") organisations by the [Communist International](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Communist_International "Communist International");[[507]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Bordiga-508) and that a [political vanguard](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vanguardism "Vanguardism") organised by [organic centralism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Organic_centralism "Organic centralism") was more effective than a vanguard organised by democratic centralism.[[508]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-509) For Bordiga and those left communists supporting his conception of Stalinism, Joseph Stalin and later Mao Zedong, [Ho Chi Minh](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ho_Chi_Minh "Ho Chi Minh"), [Che Guevara](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara "Che Guevara") and other [anti-imperialist](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anti-imperialist "Anti-imperialist") revolutionaries were great [Romantic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Romanticism "Romanticism") revolutionaries, i.e. bourgeois revolutionaries. According to this view, the Stalinist regimes that came into existence after 1945 were extending the bourgeois nature of prior revolutions that degenerated as all had in common a policy of expropriation and agrarian and productive development which those left communist considered negations of previous conditions and not the genuine construction of socialism.[[509]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-510) While the Russian Revolution was a [proletarian revolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proletarian_revolution "Proletarian revolution"), it degenerated into a [bourgeois revolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bourgeois_revolution "Bourgeois revolution")[[510]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Otto_R%C3%BChle_and_the_German_Labour_Movement-511) and represented the [French Revolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/French_Revolution "French Revolution") of the [Eastern](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eastern_world "Eastern world") and [Third World](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Third_World "Third World"), with socialism taking liberalism's place.[[507]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Bordiga-508)[[511]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-512)

Although most Marxist–Leninists distinguish between communism and socialism, Bordiga, who did consider himself a [Leninist](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Leninist "Leninist") and has been described as being "more Leninist than Lenin",[[512]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-513) did not distinguish between the two in the same way Marxist–Leninists do. Both Lenin and Bordiga did not see socialism as a separate mode of production from communism, but rather just as how communism looks as it emerges from capitalism before it has "developed on its own foundations".[[513]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-514) This is coherent with Marx and Engels, who used the terms _communism_ and _socialism_ interchangeably.[[514]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-515)[[515]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-516) Like Lenin, Bordiga used _socialism_ to mean what Marx called the "[lower-stage of communism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lower-stage_of_communism "Lower-stage of communism")".[[516]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-517) For Bordiga, both stages of communist or socialist society—with stages referring to historical materialism—were characterized by the gradual absence of money, the market and so on, the difference between them being that earlier in the first stage a system of rationing would be used to allocate goods to people while in communism this could be abandoned in favour of full free access. This view distinguished Bordiga from Marxist–Leninists, who tended and still tend to telescope the first two stages and so have money and the other exchange categories surviving into socialism, but Bordiga would have none of this. For him, no society in which money, buying and selling and the rest survived could be regarded as either socialist or communist—these exchange categories would die out before the socialist rather than the communist stage was reached.[[507]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Bordiga-508) Stalin first made the claim that the Soviet Union had reached the lower stage of communism and argued that the law of value still operated within a socialist economy.[[517]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Stalin-518)

Other left communists such as the [councilists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Councilists "Councilists") explicitly reject the Leninist vanguard party and the organic centralism promoted by [Bordigists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bordigists "Bordigists").[[518]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-519)[Otto Rühle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Otto_R%C3%BChle "Otto Rühle") saw the Soviet Union as a form of state capitalism that had much in common with the state-centred capitalism of the [West](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Western_world "Western world") as well as fascism.[[510]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Otto_R%C3%BChle_and_the_German_Labour_Movement-511) While Rühle saw the Leninist vanguardist party as an appropriate form for the overthrow of the tsarist autocracy, it was ultimately an inappropriate form for a proletarian revolution. For Rühle and others, no matter what the actual intentions of the [Bolsheviks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bolsheviks "Bolsheviks"), what they actually succeeded in bringing about was much more like the bourgeois revolutions of Europe than a proletarian revolution.[[510]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Otto_R%C3%BChle_and_the_German_Labour_Movement-511)[[519]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-520)

#### Libertarian communism and socialism

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Authoritarian_socialism&action=edit&section=36 "Edit section: Libertarian communism and socialism")]

A variety of non-state, libertarian communist and socialist positions reject the concept of a socialist state altogether, believing that the modern state is a byproduct of capitalism and cannot be used for the establishment of a socialist system. They reason that a socialist state is antithetical to socialism and that socialism will emerge spontaneously from the [grassroots](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Grassroots "Grassroots") level in an [evolutionary manner](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolutionary_economics "Evolutionary economics"), developing its own unique political and economic institutions for a highly organized [stateless society](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stateless_society "Stateless society"). Libertarian communists, including [anarchists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anarcho-communism "Anarcho-communism"), councilists, leftists and [Marxists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libertarian_Marxism "Libertarian Marxism"), also reject the concept of a socialist state for being antithetical to socialism, but they believe that socialism and communism can only be established through [revolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Revolution "Revolution") and dissolving the existence of the state.[[494]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-AFAQ_H-495)[[495]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-AFAQ_I-496) Within the socialist movement, there is criticism towards the use of the term _socialist state_ in relation to countries such as China and previously of Soviet Union and Eastern and Central European states before what some term the "collapse of Stalinism" in 1989.[[520]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-521)[[521]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-522)[[522]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-523)[[523]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-524)

Anti-authoritarian communists and socialists such as anarchists, other democratic and libertarian socialists as well as revolutionary syndicalists and left communists[[145]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-CWO_2000-145)[[524]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-525) claim that the so-called socialist states cannot be called socialist because they actually presided over state capitalist[[138]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Chomsky_1986-138)[[139]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-State_Capitalism_in_the_Soviet_Union,_2001-139)[[140]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWolff2015-140) or non-planned administrative economies.[[69]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-The_Soviet_Union_Has_an_Administered,_Not_a_Planned,_Economy,_1985-69)[[70]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Ellman_2007-70) Those socialists who oppose any system of state control whatsoever believe in a more decentralized approach which puts the means of production directly into the hands of the workers rather than indirectly through state bureaucracies[[494]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-AFAQ_H-495)[[495]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-AFAQ_I-496) which they claim represent a new elite or class.[[525]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-526)[[526]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-527)[[527]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-528)[[528]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-529)

This leads them to consider state socialism a form of state capitalism (an economy based on centralized management, capital accumulation and wage labor, but with the state owning the means of production)[[529]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-530) which Engels and other Bolshevik leaders such as Vladimir Lenin and Nikolai Bukharin stated would be the final form of capitalism rather than socialism.[[142]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-Engels-142)[[145]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-CWO_2000-145) Similarly, others pointed out that nationalisation and state ownership have nothing to do with socialism by itself, having been historically carried out for various different purposes under a wide variety of different political and economic systems.[[530]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-531)

#### Trotskyism

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Authoritarian_socialism&action=edit&section=37 "Edit section: Trotskyism")]

Some [Trotskyists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trotskyists "Trotskyists") following on from [Tony Cliff](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tony_Cliff "Tony Cliff") deny that it is socialism, calling it state capitalism.[[531]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-532) Other Trotskyists agree that these states could not be described as socialist,[[532]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-533) but they deny that those states were capitalist,[[533]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-534) supporting Leon Trotsky's analysis of pre-restoration Soviet Union as a workers' state that had [degenerated](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Degenerated_workers%27_state "Degenerated workers' state") into a bureaucratic dictatorship which rested on a largely nationalized industry run according to a plan of production.[[534]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-535)[[535]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-536)[[536]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-537) and claimed that the former Stalinist states of Central and Eastern Europe were [deformed workers' states](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deformed_workers%27_state "Deformed workers' state") based on the same relations of production as the Soviet Union.[[537]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-538)

Trotsky believed that regardless of their intellectual capacity, central planners operate without the input and participation of the millions of people who participate in the economy that can understand and respond to local conditions and changes in the economy. In advocating a [decentralised planned](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Decentralized_planning_(economics) "Decentralized planning (economics)") socialist economy, Trotsky and some of his followers have criticized central state planning as being unable to effectively coordinate all economic activity.[[538]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Authoritarian_socialism#cite_note-539)
